Well done. I created a very basic 6DOF arm a few years ago using very cheap metal gear RC servos, the problem with those is no feedback, and the shoulder torque is a bit low for good movement. I was able to connect it to ROS and control it using ROSControl joint messages. The arm was controlled by an arduino and a 16-channel servo I2C board. I have a video on vimeo somewhere of it performing some tests.

@user-borroÂ  Every engineer (electronics, mechanics, robotics) has to be able to construct robotic arm like dummy robot, because math calculations are the only complex part in the project and engineers should have good math background. Everything else (robotic arm design process, documentation, even video tutorials of how to design robotic arm) is available on the internet. Obviously the guy put in a lot of effort and time and that should be appreciated. He is lucky to have received support from Huawei because making of robotic arm is very expensive game. If you have that the price of one servo motor with harmonic drive is no less than 1,000 USD, and robotic arm has 6 joints, that is 6,000 USD. Controller electronics, mechanics parts, software licenses are expensive too. That's why industrial robotic arms are so expensive. Small robotic arm with payload and spec like this dummy robotic arm can't be found for less than 8,000 USD (if someone tell you that he has one with lower price, check it in details).

There's a bit of overselling going on in this video. He showed one tech demo after another. But for a lot of these, after a short development time, it looks like it's 90% done, and then it turns out that the remaining 10% take months to perfect. You can spend so much time finessing a single algorithm until something works exactly the way you want it to. Especially very typical with VR and AR stuff.  Plus other people obviously contributed time and money to this project. He's still very talented though, but even his days are only ever going to be 24h long, and this is either a team effort, or one that takes months and still has more problems than solutions.

@user-td1vb5ki8gÂ  I think they're both realtime capable. I was curious if he was making use of one of the P2P benefits of the CAN protocol.

CAN much stable in heavy interference envirnment. And IIC better on chip to chip in a circuit board.

@JerguuÂ  CAN bus is way more reliable in complex EMC environments, thatâ€™s why they use CAN on cars. I2C will shit itself with motors running.

iicæ˜¯åº”ç­”å¼ï¼Œè€ŒCANå¯ä»¥ä½¿å¾—æ€»çº¿ä¸Šçš„æ‰€æœ‰è®¾å¤‡éƒ½å…·æœ‰ä¸ŠæŠ¥èƒ½åŠ›

CAN bus is a lot faster than I2C comms. CAN is better for inter-device comms (i.e. to allow multiple uCs/embedded-devices to talk at high speed when there is a network of them) whereas I2C/SPI are more basic comms useful when controlling things such as I2C sensors/actuators directly connected to a specific uC (i.e. your uC needs to talk to a network of sensors). CAN is very similar to a basic network protocol, but with a small enough footprint to be useful with small-memory embedded devices. CAN has protocols for packet failure handling, retry, and resends. He is using a number of interconnected uCs in this robot, making CAN the ideal choice.

@lucysluckydayÂ   wait CAN is faster than I2C? Where did you see that

mainly because I2C is only for on board comms between chips. Whereas for inter system communication under extreme noisy conditions (EM interference generated by motor coils), CAN is perfect choice if baudrate is below 1M.
